in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <small>French:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[nɔtʁə dam də paʁi]</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cathedrale_de_Nothre_Dame.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/Cathedrale_de_Nothre_Dame.ogg" class="internal" title="Cathedrale de Nothre Dame.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; meaning "<b>Our Lady of Paris</b>"), referred to simply as <b>Notre-Dame</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> is a medieval <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedral</a> on the <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele_de_la_Cit%C3%A9" title="Île de la Cité">Île de la Cité</a> in the <a href="/wiki/4th_arrondissement_of_Paris" title="4th arrondissement of Paris">4th arrondissement of Paris</a>. The cathedral is consecrated to the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> and considered to be one of the finest examples of <a href="/wiki/French_Gothic_architecture" title="French Gothic architecture">French Gothic architecture</a>. Its pioneering use of the <a href="/wiki/Rib_vault" title="Rib vault">rib vault</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying buttress</a>, its enormous and colourful <a href="/wiki/Rose_windows" class="mw-redirect" title="Rose windows">rose windows</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(art)" class="mw-redirect" title="Naturalism (art)">naturalism</a> and abundance of its <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculptural decoration</a> set it apart from the earlier <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_style" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanesque style">Romanesque style</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDucher198846–62_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDucher198846–62-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Major components that make Notre Dame stand out include one of the world's largest organs and its immense church bells.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The cathedral's construction was begun in 1160 under Bishop <a href="/wiki/Maurice_de_Sully" title="Maurice de Sully">Maurice de Sully</a> and was largely complete by 1260, though it was modified frequently in the following centuries. In the 1790s, Notre-Dame suffered <a href="/wiki/Desecration" title="Desecration">desecration</a> during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>; much of its religious imagery was damaged or destroyed. In the 19th century, the cathedral was the site of the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Napoleon_I" title="Coronation of Napoleon I">coronation of Napoleon I</a> and funerals of many <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of France">Presidents of the Republic</a>.
</p><p>Popular interest in the cathedral blossomed soon after the publication, in 1831, of <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>'s novel <i>Notre-Dame de Paris</i> (better known in English as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hunchback_of_Notre-Dame" title="The Hunchback of Notre-Dame">The Hunchback of Notre-Dame</a></i>). This led to a major restoration project between 1844 and 1864, supervised by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Liberation_of_Paris" title="Liberation of Paris">liberation of Paris</a> was celebrated within Notre-Dame in 1944 with the singing of the <a href="/wiki/Magnificat" title="Magnificat">Magnificat</a>. Beginning in 1963, the cathedral's façade was cleaned of centuries of soot and grime. Another cleaning and restoration project was carried out between 1991 and 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis.fr_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis.fr-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The cathedral is one of the most widely recognized symbols of the city of Paris and the French nation. As the cathedral of the <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese_of_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese of Paris">Archdiocese of Paris</a>, Notre-Dame contains the <i><a href="/wiki/Cathedra" title="Cathedra">cathedra</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Archbishop of Paris">Archbishop of Paris</a> (<a href="/wiki/Michel_Aupetit" title="Michel Aupetit">Michel Aupetit</a>). In 1805, Notre-Dame was given the honorary status of a <a href="/wiki/Minor_basilica" title="Minor basilica">minor basilica</a>. Approximately 12&#160;million people visit Notre-Dame annually, making it the most visited monument in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-ParisDigest_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ParisDigest-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The cathedral is renowned for its <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a> sermons founded by the famous <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Henri_Lacordaire" title="Jean-Baptiste Henri Lacordaire">Jean-Baptiste Henri Lacordaire</a> in the 1860s. In recent years, an increasing number have been given by leading public figures and state employed academics.
</p><p>The cathedral has been progressively stripped of its original decoration and works of art. Several noteworthy examples of Gothic, Baroque, and 19th-century sculptures and a group of 17th- and early 18th-century altarpieces remain in the cathedral's collection. Some of the most important relics in Christendom, including the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Thorns" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown of Thorns">Crown of Thorns</a>, a <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">sliver of the true cross</a> and a nail from the true cross, are preserved at Notre-Dame.
</p><p>While undergoing renovation and restoration, the roof of Notre-Dame <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris_fire" title="Notre-Dame de Paris fire">caught fire</a> on the evening of 15 April 2019. Burning for around 15 hours, the cathedral sustained serious damage, including the destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Fl%C3%A8che" title="Flèche">flèche</a> (the timber <a href="/wiki/Spire" title="Spire">spire</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Crossing_(architecture)" title="Crossing (architecture)">crossing</a>) and most of the lead-covered wooden roof above the stone <a href="/wiki/Vaulting" class="mw-redirect" title="Vaulting">vaulted</a> ceiling.<sup id="cite_ref-partofspirecollapses_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-partofspirecollapses-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Contamination of the site and the nearby environment resulted.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Following the April fire, many proposals were made for modernizing the cathedral's design. However, on 16 July 2019, the <a href="/wiki/French_Parliament" title="French Parliament">French Parliament</a> passed a law requiring that it be rebuilt exactly as it appeared before the fire.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It is believed that before the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianity in France">Christianity in France</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Roman" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallo-Roman">Gallo-Roman</a> temple dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a> stood on the site of Notre-Dame. Evidence for this is the <a href="/wiki/Pillar_of_the_Boatmen" title="Pillar of the Boatmen">Pillar of the Boatmen</a>, discovered in 1710. This building was replaced with an <a href="/wiki/Early_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christian">Early Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a>. It is unknown whether this church, dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Stephen" title="Saint Stephen">Saint Stephen</a>, was constructed in the late 4th century and remodeled later, or if it was built in the 7th century from an older church, possibly the cathedral of <a href="/wiki/Childebert_I" title="Childebert I">Childebert I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> The basilica, later <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cathedral_of_Saint-Etienne,_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cathedral of Saint-Etienne, Paris (page does not exist)">cathedral, of Saint-Étienne</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cath%C3%A9drale_Saint-%C3%89tienne_de_Paris" class="extiw" title="fr:Cathédrale Saint-Étienne de Paris">fr</a>&#93;</span> was situated about 40 meters (130&#160;ft) west of Notre-Dame's location and was wider and lower and roughly half its size. For its time, it was very large—70 meters (230&#160;ft) long—and separated into <a href="/wiki/Nave_(architecture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nave (architecture)">nave</a> and four <a href="/wiki/Aisle" title="Aisle">aisles</a> by marble columns, then decorated with <a href="/wiki/Mosaics" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosaics">mosaics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis.fr_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis.fr-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PREMIERES_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PREMIERES-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Four churches succeeded the Roman temple before Notre-Dame. The first was the 4th century basilica of Saint-Étienne, then the <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Merovingian architecture">Merovingian</a> renovation of that church which was in turn remodeled in 857 under the <a href="/wiki/Carolingians" class="mw-redirect" title="Carolingians">Carolingians</a> into a cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMortet18885–6_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMortet18885–6-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The last church before the cathedral of Notre-Dame was a <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> remodeling of the prior structures that, although enlarged and remodeled, was found to be unfit for the growing population of Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-lepoint_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lepoint-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Baptistery" title="Baptistery">baptistery</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Baptistery_Church_of_John_the_Baptist,_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Baptistery Church of John the Baptist, Paris (page does not exist)">the Church of John the Baptist</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%89glise_Saint-Jean-le-Rond_de_Paris" class="extiw" title="fr:Église Saint-Jean-le-Rond de Paris">fr</a>&#93;</span>, built before 452, was located on the north side of the church of Saint-Étienne until the work of <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Germain_Soufflot" title="Jacques-Germain Soufflot">Jacques-Germain Soufflot</a> in the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-rouche_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rouche-20">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1160, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Paris">Bishop of Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_de_Sully" title="Maurice de Sully">Maurice de Sully</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-rouche_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rouche-20">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> decided to build a new and much larger church. He summarily demolished the Romanesque cathedral and chose to recycle its materials.<sup id="cite_ref-lepoint_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lepoint-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Sully decided that the new church should be built in the <a href="/wiki/French_Gothic_architecture" title="French Gothic architecture">Gothic style</a>, which had been inaugurated at the royal abbey of Saint Denis in the late 1130s.<sup id="cite_ref-PREMIERES_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PREMIERES-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction">Construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Notre-Dame_de_Paris-France.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Notre-Dame_de_Paris-France.JPG/310px-Notre-Dame_de_Paris-France.JPG" decoding="async" width="310" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Notre-Dame_de_Paris-France.JPG/465px-Notre-Dame_de_Paris-France.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Notre-Dame_de_Paris-France.JPG/620px-Notre-Dame_de_Paris-France.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1008" data-file-height="623" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Notre-Dame_de_Paris-France.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Notre-Dame de Paris (2011).</div></div></div>
<p>The chronicler <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jean_de_Saint-Victor_(chronicler)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jean de Saint-Victor (chronicler) (page does not exist)">Jean de Saint-Victor</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_de_Saint-Victor_(chroniqueur)" class="extiw" title="fr:Jean de Saint-Victor (chroniqueur)">fr</a>&#93;</span> recorded in the <i>Memorial Historiarum</i> that the construction of Notre-Dame began between 24 March and 25 April 1163 with the laying of the <a href="/wiki/Cornerstone" title="Cornerstone">cornerstone</a> in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Louis_VII_of_France" title="Louis VII of France">King Louis VII</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_III" title="Pope Alexander III">Pope Alexander III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Villefosse198025_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Villefosse198025-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenriet2005294_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenriet2005294-22">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Four phases of construction took place under bishops Maurice de Sully and <a href="/wiki/Eudes_de_Sully" title="Eudes de Sully">Eudes de Sully</a> (not related to Maurice), according to masters whose names have been lost.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png/220px-Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="322" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png/330px-Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png/440px-Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png 2x" data-file-width="716" data-file-height="1047" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coupe.transversale.cathedrale.Paris.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross-section of the double supporting arches and buttresses of the <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a>, drawn by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a> as they would have appeared from 1220 to 1230.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEViollet-le-Duc1868288_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEViollet-le-Duc1868288-23">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The first phase began with the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Choir_(architecture)" title="Choir (architecture)">choir</a> and its two <a href="/wiki/Ambulatory" title="Ambulatory">ambulatories</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/Robert_of_Torigni" title="Robert of Torigni">Robert of Torigni</a>, the choir was completed in 1177 and the <a href="/wiki/High_altar" class="mw-redirect" title="High altar">high altar</a> consecrated on 19 May 1182 by Cardinal Henri de Château-Marçay, the Papal legate in Paris, and Maurice de Sully.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDelisle187368_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDelisle187368-24">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The second phase, from 1182 to 1190, concerned the construction of the four sections of the nave behind the choir and its aisles to the height of the <a href="/wiki/Clerestories" class="mw-redirect" title="Clerestories">clerestories</a>. It began after the completion of the choir but ended before the final allotted section of the nave was finished. Beginning in 1190, the bases of the façade were put in place, and the first traverses were completed.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis.fr_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis.fr-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heraclius_of_Caesarea" class="mw-redirect" title="Heraclius of Caesarea">Heraclius of Caesarea</a> called for the <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Third Crusade</a> in 1185 from the still-incomplete cathedral.
</p><p>Louis IX deposited the relics of the passion of Christ, which included the crown of thorns, a nail from the cross and a sliver of the cross, which he had purchased at great expense from the Latin Emperor Baudouin II, in the cathedral during the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Chapelle" title="Sainte-Chapelle">Sainte-Chapelle</a>.
</p><p>The decision was made to add a <a href="/wiki/Transepts" class="mw-redirect" title="Transepts">transepts</a> at the choir, where the altar was located, in order to bring more light into the center of the church. The use of simpler four-part rather than six-part rib vaults meant that the roofs were stronger and could be higher. After Bishop Maurice de Sully's death in 1196, his successor, <a href="/wiki/Eudes_de_Sully" title="Eudes de Sully">Eudes de Sully</a> oversaw the completion of the transepts, and continued work on the <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a>, which was nearing completion at the time of his own death in 1208. By this time, the western façade was already largely built, though it was not completed until around the mid-1240s. Between 1225 and 1250 the upper gallery of the nave was constructed, along with the two towers on the west façade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruzelius1987540–69_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruzelius1987540–69-25">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another significant change came in the mid-13th century, when the transepts were remodeled in the latest <a href="/wiki/Rayonnant" title="Rayonnant">Rayonnant</a> style; in the late 1240s <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_Chelles" title="Jean de Chelles">Jean de Chelles</a> added a gabled portal to the north transept topped off by a spectacular rose window. Shortly afterward (from 1258) <a href="/wiki/Peter_of_Montereau" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter of Montereau">Pierre de Montreuil</a> executed a similar scheme on the southern transept. Both these transept portals were richly embellished with sculpture; the south portal features scenes from the lives of St Stephen and of various local saints, while the north portal featured the infancy of Christ and the story of Theophilus in the tympanum, with a highly influential statue of the Virgin and Child in the <a href="/wiki/Trumeau_(architecture)" title="Trumeau (architecture)">trumeau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliamson1995_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliamson1995-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruzelius1987540–69_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruzelius1987540–69-25">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Master builders <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Chelles" title="Pierre de Chelles">Pierre de Chelles</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jean_Ravy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jean Ravy (page does not exist)">Jean Ravy</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_Ravy" class="extiw" title="fr:Jean Ravy">fr</a>&#93;</span>, Jean le Bouteiller, and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Raymond_du_Temple&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Raymond du Temple (page does not exist)">Raymond du Temple</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raymond_du_Temple" class="extiw" title="fr:Raymond du Temple">fr</a>&#93;</span> succeeded de Chelles and de Montreuil and then each other in the construction of the cathedral. Ravy completed de Chelles's <a href="/wiki/Rood_screen" title="Rood screen">rood screen</a> and chevet chapels, then began the 15-metre (49&#160;ft) flying buttresses of the choir. Jean le Bouteiller, Ravy's nephew, succeeded him in 1344 and was himself replaced on his death in 1363 by his deputy, Raymond du Temple.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip the Fair</a> opened the first <a href="/wiki/Estates_General_(France)" title="Estates General (France)">Estates General</a> in the cathedral in 1302.
</p><p>An important innovation in the 13th century was the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Flying_buttress" title="Flying buttress">flying buttress</a>. Before the buttresses, all of the weight of the roof pressed outward and down to the walls, and the abutments supporting them. With the flying buttress, the weight was carried by the ribs of the vault entirely outside the structure to a series of counter-supports, which were topped with stone pinnacles which gave them greater weight. The buttresses meant that the walls could be higher and thinner, and could have much larger windows. The date of the first buttresses is not known with any great precision beyond an installation date in the 13th century. Art historian <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Tallon" title="Andrew Tallon">Andrew Tallon</a>, however, has argued based on detailed laser scans of the entire structure that the buttresses were part of the original design. According to Tallon, the scans indicate that "the upper part of the building has not moved one smidgen in 800 years,"<sup id="cite_ref-Tallon_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tallon-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> whereas if they were added later some movement from prior to their addition would be expected. Tallon thus concluded that "flying buttresses were there from the get-go."<sup id="cite_ref-Tallon_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tallon-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The first buttresses were replaced by larger and stronger ones in the 14th century; these had a reach of fifteen metres between the walls and counter-supports.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis.fr_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis.fr-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

Portals and nave to the left, a choir in the center, and <a href="/wiki/Apse" title="Apse">apse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ambulatory" title="Ambulatory">ambulatory</a> to the right. The annex to the south is the <a href="/wiki/Sacristy" title="Sacristy">Sacristy</a>.
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 249.33333333333px"><div style="width: 249.33333333333px">

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 232px"><div style="width: 232px">

</p>

</p>
			</div>

			<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_of_Jandun" title="John of Jandun">John of Jandun</a> recognized the cathedral as one of Paris's three most important buildings [prominent structures] in his 1323 <i>Treatise on the Praises of Paris</i>:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>That most glorious church of the most glorious Virgin Mary, mother of God, deservedly shines out, like the sun among stars. And although some speakers, by their own free judgment, because [they are] able to see only a few things easily, may say that some other is more beautiful, I believe, however, respectfully, that, if they attend more diligently to the whole and the parts, they will quickly retract this opinion. Where indeed, I ask, would they find two towers of such magnificence and perfection, so high, so large, so strong, clothed round about with such multiple varieties of ornaments? Where, I ask, would they find such a multipartite arrangement of so many lateral vaults, above and below? Where, I ask, would they find such light-filled amenities as the many surrounding chapels? Furthermore, let them tell me in what church I may see such a large cross, of which one arm separates the choir from the nave. Finally, I would willingly learn where [there are] two such circles, situated opposite each other in a straight line, which on account of their appearance are given the name of the fourth vowel [O]&#160;; among which smaller orbs and circles, with wondrous artifice, so that some arranged circularly, others angularly, surround windows ruddy with precious colors and beautiful with the most subtle figures of the pictures. In fact, I believe that this church offers the carefully discerning such cause for admiration that its inspection can scarcely sate the soul.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Jean de Jandun, Tractatus de laudibus Parisius<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInglis200363–85_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInglis200363–85-28">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sacre_Henry6_England-France_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Sacre_Henry6_England-France_02.jpg/220px-Sacre_Henry6_England-France_02.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Sacre_Henry6_England-France_02.jpg/330px-Sacre_Henry6_England-France_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Sacre_Henry6_England-France_02.jpg/440px-Sacre_Henry6_England-France_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3420" data-file-height="3304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sacre_Henry6_England-France_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">Henry VI of England</a>'s coronation as King of France, aged ten, during the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years&#39; War">Hundred Years' War</a>. His accession to the throne was in accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Troyes" title="Treaty of Troyes">Treaty of Troyes</a> of 1420.</div></div></div>
<p>On 16 December 1431, the boy-king <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">Henry VI of England</a> was crowned King of France in Notre-Dame, aged ten, the traditional coronation church of <a href="/wiki/Reims_Cathedral" title="Reims Cathedral">Reims Cathedral</a> being under French control.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, the Gothic style fell out of style, and the internal pillars and walls of Notre-Dame were covered with tapestries.<sup id="cite_ref-Libération1_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Libération1-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1548, rioting <a href="/wiki/Huguenots" title="Huguenots">Huguenots</a> damaged some of the statues of Notre-Dame, considering them <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatrous</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chavis_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavis-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_Parvise_Fountain&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Notre-Dame Parvise Fountain (page does not exist)">The fountain</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fontaine_du_Parvis_Notre-Dame" class="extiw" title="fr:Fontaine du Parvis Notre-Dame">fr</a>&#93;</span> in Notre-Dame's <a href="/wiki/Parvise" title="Parvise">parvis</a> was added in 1625 to provide nearby Parisians with running water.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">King Louis XIV</a>, on the insistence of his father, <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIII_of_France" title="Louis XIII of France">Louis XIII</a>, decided in 1699 to make extensive modifications to Notre-Dame. He tasked <a href="/wiki/Robert_de_Cotte" title="Robert de Cotte">Robert de Cotte</a> with the renovation. Cotte replaced the rood screen with a sumptuous and gilded <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> fence, opened up the choir and ambulatory, and removed the tombs in the nave. New furniture was produced as well as the current high altar, depicting Louis XIV and XIII kneeling before a <i><a href="/wiki/Piet%C3%A0" title="Pietà">Pietà</a></i>.
</p><p>Since 1449, the Parisian goldsmith guild had made regular donations to the cathedral chapter. In 1630, it was decided that the guild would donate a large altarpiece every year on the first of May. These works came to be known as the <i><a href="/wiki/May_(painting)" title="May (painting)">grands mays</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The subject matter was restricted to episodes from the Acts of the Apostles. The prestigious commission was awarded to the most prominent painters and, after 1648, members of the Academie royale.
</p><p>Seventy-six paintings had been donated by 1708, when the custom was discontinued for financial reasons. Those works were confiscated in 1793 and the majority were subsequently dispersed among regional museums in France. Those that remained in the cathedral were removed or relocated within the building by the 19th-century restorers.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anjou_paris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Anjou_paris.jpg/220px-Anjou_paris.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Anjou_paris.jpg/330px-Anjou_paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Anjou_paris.jpg/440px-Anjou_paris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3528" data-file-height="3850" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anjou_paris.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cathedral of Notre Dame in the <a href="/wiki/Paris_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Paris in the Middle Ages">15th century</a>, illuminated by the <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Anthony_of_Burgundy" title="Master of Anthony of Burgundy">Master of Anthony of Burgundy</a></div></div></div>
<p>Thirteen of the <i>grands mays</i> remain in Nôtre Dame:
</p><p><i>La Descente du Saint Esprit</i> by Jacques Blanchard – 1634
</p><p><i>Saint Pierre guérissant les malades de son ombre</i> by Laurent de la Hyre – 1635
</p><p><i>La Conversion de saint Paul</i> by Laurent de la Hyre – 1637
</p><p><i>Le Centenier Corneille aux pieds de saint Pierre</i> by Aubin Vouet – 1639
</p><p><i>La Prédication de saint Pierre à Jérusalem</i> by Charles Poerson – 1642
</p><p><i>Le Crucifiement de saint Pierre</i> by Sébastien Bourdon – 1643
</p><p><i>Le Crucifiement de saint André</i> by Charles Le Brun – 1647
</p><p><i>Saint Paul rend aveugle le faux prophète Barjesu et convertit le proconsul Sergius</i> by Nicolas Loir – 1650
</p><p><i>La Lapidation de saint Étienne</i> by Charles Le Brun – 1651
</p><p><i>La Flagellation de Saint Paul et Silas</i> by Louis Testelin – 1655
</p><p><i>Saint André tressaille de joie à la vue de son supplice</i>par by Gabriel Blanchard – 1670
</p><p><i>Le Prophète Agabus prédisant à saint Paul ses souffrances à Jérusalem</i> by Louis Chéron – 1687
</p><p><i>Les fils de Sceva battus par le démon</i> by Mathieu Elyas – 1702
</p><p>These paintings suffered water damage during the fire of 2019 and were removed for conservation.
</p><p>An altarpiece depicting the <i>Visitation,</i> painted by Jean Jouvenet in 1707, was also located in the cathedral.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Canon_(priest)" title="Canon (priest)">canon</a> Antoine de La Porte commissioned for Louis XIV six paintings depicting the life of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> for the choir. At this same time, <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_La_Fosse" title="Charles de La Fosse">Charles de La Fosse</a> painted his <i>Adoration of the Magi</i>, now in the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Louis_Antoine_de_Noailles" title="Louis Antoine de Noailles">Louis Antoine de Noailles</a>, Archbishop of Paris, extensively modified the roof of Notre-Dame in 1726, renovating its framing and removing the gargoyles with lead gutters. Noailles also strengthened the buttresses, galleries, terraces, and vaults.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELassusViollet-le-Duc184318_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELassusViollet-le-Duc184318-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In 1756, the cathedral's canons decided that its interior was too dark. The medieval stained glass windows, except the rosettes, were removed and replaced with plain, white glass panes.<sup id="cite_ref-Libération1_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Libération1-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Finally, <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Germain_Soufflot" title="Jacques-Germain Soufflot">Jacques-Germain Soufflot</a> was tasked with the modification of the portals at the front of the cathedral to allow processions to more easily enter it.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="French_Revolution_and_Napoleon">French Revolution and Napoleon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: French Revolution and Napoleon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Percier_et_Fontaine_004.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Percier_et_Fontaine_004.jpg/250px-Percier_et_Fontaine_004.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Percier_et_Fontaine_004.jpg/375px-Percier_et_Fontaine_004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Percier_et_Fontaine_004.jpg/500px-Percier_et_Fontaine_004.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1300" data-file-height="908" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Percier_et_Fontaine_004.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arrival of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> at Notre-Dame Cathedral for his <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Napoleon_I" title="Coronation of Napoleon I">coronation</a> as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_the_French" title="Emperor of the French">Emperor of the French</a> on 2 December 1804</div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> in 1789, Notre-Dame and the rest of the clergy's property in France was seized and made public property.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchives_parlementaires,_Vol._22202–05_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchives_parlementaires,_Vol._22202–05-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The cathedral was rededicated in 1793 to the <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_Reason" title="Cult of Reason">Cult of Reason</a>, and then to the <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_the_Supreme_Being" title="Cult of the Supreme Being">Cult of the Supreme Being</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuvergier1825281_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuvergier1825281-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> During this time, many of the treasures of the cathedral were either destroyed or plundered. The twenty-eight statues of biblical kings located at the west façade, mistaken for statues of French kings, were beheaded.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis.fr_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis.fr-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alexander_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Many of the heads were found during a 1977 excavation nearby, and are on display at the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_Cluny" title="Musée de Cluny">Musée de Cluny</a>. For a time the <a href="/wiki/Liberty_(personification)" title="Liberty (personification)">Goddess of Liberty</a> replaced the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> on several altars.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrick200475–76_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrick200475–76-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The cathedral's great bells escaped being melted down. All of the other large statues on the façade, with the exception of the statue of the Virgin Mary on the portal of the cloister, were destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis.fr_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis.fr-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The cathedral came to be used as a warehouse for the storage of food and other non-religious purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-Chavis_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavis-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1801" title="Concordat of 1801">Concordat of 1801</a>, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> restored Notre-Dame to the Catholic Church, though this was only finalized on 18 April 1802. Napoleon also named Paris's new bishop, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_de_Belloy" title="Jean-Baptiste de Belloy">Jean-Baptiste de Belloy</a>, who restored the cathedral's interior. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Percier" title="Charles Percier">Charles Percier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Fran%C3%A7ois-L%C3%A9onard_Fontaine" title="Pierre-François-Léonard Fontaine">Pierre-François-Léonard Fontaine</a> made quasi-Gothic modifications to Notre-Dame for the coronation of Napoleon as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_the_French" title="Emperor of the French">Emperor of the French</a> within the cathedral. The building's exterior was <a href="/wiki/Whitewash" title="Whitewash">whitewashed</a> and the interior decorated in <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical</a>, then in vogue.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration">Restoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Restoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bayard,_Hippolyte_-_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_(2)_(Zeno_Fotografie).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Bayard%2C_Hippolyte_-_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_%282%29_%28Zeno_Fotografie%29.jpg/280px-Bayard%2C_Hippolyte_-_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_%282%29_%28Zeno_Fotografie%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Bayard%2C_Hippolyte_-_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_%282%29_%28Zeno_Fotografie%29.jpg/420px-Bayard%2C_Hippolyte_-_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_%282%29_%28Zeno_Fotografie%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Bayard%2C_Hippolyte_-_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_%282%29_%28Zeno_Fotografie%29.jpg/560px-Bayard%2C_Hippolyte_-_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_%282%29_%28Zeno_Fotografie%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2024" data-file-height="1489" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bayard,_Hippolyte_-_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_(2)_(Zeno_Fotografie).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cathedral at the beginning of the restoration work, photo from 1847 by <a href="/wiki/Hippolyte_Bayard" title="Hippolyte Bayard">Hippolyte Bayard</a></div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, Notre-Dame was in such a state of disrepair that Paris officials considered its demolition. <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>, who admired the cathedral, wrote the novel <i>Notre-Dame de Paris</i> (published in English as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hunchback_of_Notre-Dame" title="The Hunchback of Notre-Dame">The Hunchback of Notre-Dame</a></i>) in 1831 to save Notre-Dame. The book was an enormous success, raising awareness of the cathedral's decaying state.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis.fr_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis.fr-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The same year as Hugo's novel was published, however, anti-<a href="/wiki/Legitimists" title="Legitimists">Legitimists</a> plundered Notre-Dame's <a href="/wiki/Sacristy" title="Sacristy">sacristy</a> and broke its stained glass windows.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 1844 King <a href="/wiki/Louis_Philippe" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis Philippe">Louis Philippe</a> ordered that the church be restored.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis.fr_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis.fr-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Notre-Dame_Spire_Model_(1859).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Notre-Dame_Spire_Model_%281859%29.jpg/200px-Notre-Dame_Spire_Model_%281859%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Notre-Dame_Spire_Model_%281859%29.jpg/300px-Notre-Dame_Spire_Model_%281859%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Notre-Dame_Spire_Model_%281859%29.jpg/400px-Notre-Dame_Spire_Model_%281859%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1936" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Notre-Dame_Spire_Model_(1859).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model of the spire and wooden roof made for <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Viollet-le-Duc" class="mw-redirect" title="Eugene Viollet-le-Duc">Eugene Viollet-le-Duc</a> (1859) (Museum of Historic Monuments, Paris)</div></div></div>
<p>The architect who had hitherto been in charge of Notre-Dame's maintenance, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne-Hippolyte_Godde" title="Étienne-Hippolyte Godde">Étienne-Hippolyte Godde</a>, was dismissed. In his stead, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Lassus" title="Jean-Baptiste Lassus">Jean-Baptiste Lassus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a>, who had distinguished themselves with the restoration of the nearby <a href="/wiki/Sainte-Chapelle" title="Sainte-Chapelle">Sainte-Chapelle</a>, were appointed in 1844. The next year, Viollet-le-Duc submitted a budget of 3,888,500 <a href="/wiki/French_franc" title="French franc">francs</a>, which was reduced to 2,650,000 francs, for the restoration of Notre-Dame and the construction of a new sacristy building. This budget was exhausted in 1850, and work stopped as Viollet-le-Duc made proposals for more money. In totality, the restoration cost over 12 million francs. When Lassus died in 1857, Viollet-le-Duc was left sole architect of the project until its completion on 31 May 1864.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Supervising a large team of sculptors, glass makers and other craftsmen, and working from drawings or engravings, Viollet-le-Duc remade or added decorations if he felt they were in the spirit of the original style. One of the latter items was a taller and more ornate <a href="/wiki/Spire" title="Spire">spire</a>, to replace the original 13th century spire, which had been removed in 1786.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The decoration of the restoration included a statue of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_the_Apostle" title="Thomas the Apostle">Saint Thomas</a> that resembles Viollet-le-Duc, as well as the sculpture of mythical creatures on the <i>Galerie des Chimères</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chavis_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavis-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The construction of the sacristy was especially financially costly. To secure a firm foundation, it was necessary for Viollet-le-Duc's laborers to dig 9 meters (30&#160;ft). Master glassworkers meticulously copied styles of the 13th century, as written about by art historians Antoine Lusson and <a href="/wiki/Adolphe_Napol%C3%A9on_Didron" title="Adolphe Napoléon Didron">Adolphe Napoléon Didron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECabezas1988118–20_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECabezas1988118–20-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Point_Z%C3%A9ro_des_Routes_de_France_(1).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Point_Z%C3%A9ro_des_Routes_de_France_%281%29.JPG/200px-Point_Z%C3%A9ro_des_Routes_de_France_%281%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Point_Z%C3%A9ro_des_Routes_de_France_%281%29.JPG/300px-Point_Z%C3%A9ro_des_Routes_de_France_%281%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Point_Z%C3%A9ro_des_Routes_de_France_%281%29.JPG/400px-Point_Z%C3%A9ro_des_Routes_de_France_%281%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Point_Z%C3%A9ro_des_Routes_de_France_(1).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French road system's <i>Point Zéro</i> spot on the ground in front of the Notre Dame (since 1924).</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antonieta_Rivas_Mercado" title="Antonieta Rivas Mercado">Antonieta Rivas Mercado</a> shot herself at the altar of Notre-Dame on 11 February 1931 with a pistol that was the property of her lover <a href="/wiki/Jose_Vasconcelos" class="mw-redirect" title="Jose Vasconcelos">Jose Vasconcelos</a>. She died instantly.
</p><p>During the liberation of Paris in August 1944, the cathedral suffered some minor damage from stray bullets. Some of the medieval glass was damaged, and was replaced by glass with modern abstract designs. On 26 August, a special mass was held in the cathedral to celebrate the liberation of Paris from the Germans; it was attended by General <a href="/wiki/Charles_De_Gaulle" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles De Gaulle">Charles De Gaulle</a> and General <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Leclerc_de_Hauteclocque" title="Philippe Leclerc de Hauteclocque">Philippe Leclerc</a>.
</p><p>In 1963, on the initiative of culture minister <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Malraux" title="André Malraux">André Malraux</a> and to mark the 800th anniversary of the Cathedral, the façade was cleaned of the centuries of soot and grime, restoring it to its original off-white color.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%C3%89douard_Baldus,_Notre-Dame_(Abside),_1860s_-_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/%C3%89douard_Baldus%2C_Notre-Dame_%28Abside%29%2C_1860s_-_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art.jpg/280px-%C3%89douard_Baldus%2C_Notre-Dame_%28Abside%29%2C_1860s_-_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/%C3%89douard_Baldus%2C_Notre-Dame_%28Abside%29%2C_1860s_-_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art.jpg/420px-%C3%89douard_Baldus%2C_Notre-Dame_%28Abside%29%2C_1860s_-_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/%C3%89douard_Baldus%2C_Notre-Dame_%28Abside%29%2C_1860s_-_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art.jpg/560px-%C3%89douard_Baldus%2C_Notre-Dame_%28Abside%29%2C_1860s_-_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3807" data-file-height="2942" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%C3%89douard_Baldus,_Notre-Dame_(Abside),_1860s_-_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apse of Notre-Dame in the 1860s.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Requiem_Mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Requiem Mass">Requiem Mass</a> of <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> was held in Notre-Dame on 12 November 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The next year, on 26 June 1971, <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Petit" title="Philippe Petit">Philippe Petit</a> walked across a tight-rope strung up between Notre-Dame's two bell towers and entertained spectators.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Magnificat" title="Magnificat">Magnificat</a> of 30 May 1980, <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a> celebrates Mass on the <a href="/wiki/Parvis" class="mw-redirect" title="Parvis">parvis</a> of the cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Requiem Mass of <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Mitterrand" title="François Mitterrand">François Mitterrand</a> was held at the cathedral, as with past French heads of state, on 11 January 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The stone masonry of the cathedral's exterior had deteriorated in the 19th and 20th century due to increased <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> in Paris, which accelerated erosion of decorations and discolored the stone. By the late 1980s, several gargoyles and turrets had also fallen off or become too loose to remain in place.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-1992_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-1992-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> A decade-long renovation program began in 1991 and replaced much of the exterior, with care given to retain the authentic architectural elements of the cathedral, including rigorous inspection of new limestone blocks.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-1992_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-1992-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> A discreet system of electrical wires, not visible from below, was also installed on the roof to deter pigeons.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The cathedral's <a href="/wiki/Pipe_organ" title="Pipe organ">pipe organ</a> was upgraded with a computerized system to control the mechanical connections to the <a href="/wiki/Organ_pipe" title="Organ pipe">pipes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The west face was cleaned and restored in time for <a href="/wiki/Millennium_celebrations" title="Millennium celebrations">millennium celebrations</a> in December 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NOTRE_DAME_DE_PARIS_May_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/NOTRE_DAME_DE_PARIS_May_2012.jpg/220px-NOTRE_DAME_DE_PARIS_May_2012.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/NOTRE_DAME_DE_PARIS_May_2012.jpg/330px-NOTRE_DAME_DE_PARIS_May_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/NOTRE_DAME_DE_PARIS_May_2012.jpg/440px-NOTRE_DAME_DE_PARIS_May_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="888" data-file-height="1296" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NOTRE_DAME_DE_PARIS_May_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Notre-Dame in May 2012. From top to bottom, <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">nave</a> walls are pierced by <a href="/wiki/Clerestory" title="Clerestory">clerestory</a> windows, arches to <a href="/wiki/Triforium" title="Triforium">triforium</a>, and arches to <a href="/wiki/Side_aisle" class="mw-redirect" title="Side aisle">side aisles</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Requiem Mass of Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Jean-Marie_Lustiger" title="Jean-Marie Lustiger">Jean-Marie Lustiger</a>, former Archbishop of Paris and famous Jewish convert to Catholicism, was held in Notre-Dame on 10 August 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The set of four 19th-century bells atop the northern towers at Notre-Dame were melted down and recast into new bronze bells in 2013, to celebrate the building's 850th anniversary. They were designed to recreate the sound of the cathedral's original bells from the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Despite the 1990s renovation, the cathedral had continued to show signs of deterioration that prompted the national government to propose a new renovation program in the late 2010s.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The entire renovation was estimated to cost €100&#160;million, which the Archbishop of Paris planned to raise through funds from the national government and private donations.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> A €6&#160;million renovation of the cathedral's spire began in late 2018 and continued into the following year, requiring the temporary removal of copper statues on the roof and other decorative elements days before the April 2019 fire.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Notre-Dame began a year-long celebration of the 850th anniversary of the laying of the first building block for the cathedral on 12 December 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> During that anniversary year, on 21 May 2013, <a href="/wiki/Dominique_Venner" title="Dominique Venner">Dominique Venner</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">historian</a> and white nationalist, placed a letter on the <a href="/wiki/Church_(building)" title="Church (building)">Church</a> <a href="/wiki/Altar" title="Altar">altar</a> and shot himself, dying instantly. Around 1,500 visitors were evacuated from the cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>French police arrested two persons on 8 September 2016 <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris_bombing_attempt" title="Notre-Dame de Paris bombing attempt">after a car containing seven gas canisters was found near Notre-Dame</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 10 February 2017, French police arrested four persons in <a href="/wiki/Montpellier" title="Montpellier">Montpellier</a> already known by authorities to have ties to radical Islamist organizations on charges of plotting to travel to Paris and attack the cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Later that year, on 6 June, visitors were shut inside Notre-Dame cathedral in Paris after <a href="/wiki/2017_Notre-Dame_de_Paris_attack" title="2017 Notre-Dame de Paris attack">a man with a hammer attacked a police officer outside</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2019_fire">2019 fire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2019 fire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris_fire" title="Notre-Dame de Paris fire">Notre-Dame de Paris fire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Incendie_Notre_Dame_de_Paris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Incendie_Notre_Dame_de_Paris.jpg/220px-Incendie_Notre_Dame_de_Paris.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Incendie_Notre_Dame_de_Paris.jpg/330px-Incendie_Notre_Dame_de_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Incendie_Notre_Dame_de_Paris.jpg/440px-Incendie_Notre_Dame_de_Paris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="4032" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Incendie_Notre_Dame_de_Paris.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 2019 fire destroyed the roof and spire but left the structure intact</div></div></div>
<p>On 15 April 2019 the cathedral caught fire, destroying the spire and the <a href="/wiki/Frame_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Frame of Notre-Dame de Paris">oak frame</a> and lead roof.<sup id="cite_ref-partofspirecollapses_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-partofspirecollapses-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fire1_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fire1-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fire2_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fire2-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> It was speculated that the fire was linked to ongoing renovation work.
</p><p>According to later studies, the fire broke out in the attic of the cathedral at 18:18. The smoke detectors immediately signaled the fire to a cathedral employee, who did not summon the fire brigade but instead sent a cathedral guard to investigate. Instead of going to the attic, the guard was sent to the wrong location, to the attic of the adjoining sacristy, and reported there was no fire. The guard telephoned his supervisor, who did not immediately answer. About fifteen minutes later the error was discovered, whereupon the guard's supervisor told him to go to the correct location. The fire brigade was still not notified. By the time guard had climbed the three hundred steps to the cathedral attic the fire was well advanced.<sup id="cite_ref-New_York_Times_2019_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_York_Times_2019-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The alarm system was not designed to automatically notify the fire brigade, which was finally summoned at 18:51 after the guard had returned from the attic and reported a now-raging fire, and more than half an hour after the fire alarm had begun sounding.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes20190419_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes20190419-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
Firefighters arrived in less than ten minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The spire of the cathedral collapsed at 19:50, bringing down some 750 tons of stone and lead.  The firemen inside were ordered back down. By this time the fire had spread to the north tower, where the eight bells were located.  The firefighters concentrated their efforts in the tower.  They feared that,  if the bells fell, they could wreck the tower, and endanger the structure of the other tower and the whole cathedral.  They had to ascend a stairway threatened by fire, and to contend with low water pressure for their hoses.  As other firemen watered the stairway and the roof, a team of twenty firemen climbed up the narrow stairway of the south tower, crossed to the north tower, lowered hoses to be connected to fire engines outside the cathedral, and sprayed water on the fire beneath the bells.  By 21:45, they were finally able to bring the fire under control.<sup id="cite_ref-New_York_Times_2019_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_York_Times_2019-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
The main structure was intact; firefighters saved the façade, towers, walls, buttresses, and stained glass windows. The Great Organ, which has over 8,000 <a href="/wiki/Organ_pipe" title="Organ pipe">pipes</a> and was built by François Thierry in the 18th century was also saved but sustained water damage.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Because of the ongoing renovation, the copper statues on the spire had been removed before the fire.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The stone vaulting that forms the ceiling of the cathedral had several holes but was otherwise intact.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 1905, France's cathedrals (including Notre-Dame) have been owned by the state, which is self-insured. Some costs might be recovered through insurance coverage if the fire is found to have been caused by <a href="/wiki/General_contractor" title="General contractor">contractors</a> working on the site.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The French insurer <a href="/wiki/AXA" title="AXA">AXA</a> provided insurance coverage for two of the contracting firms working on Notre-Dame's restoration before the blaze which devastated the cathedral. AXA also provided insurance coverage for some of the relics and artworks in the cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>President <a href="/wiki/Emmanuel_Macron" title="Emmanuel Macron">Emmanuel Macron</a> said approximately 500 firefighters helped to battle the fire. One firefighter was seriously injured and two police officers were hurt during the blaze.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>  
</p><p>An ornate tapestry woven in the early 1800s is going on public display for only the third time in recent decades. The decoration was rescued from Notre-Dame de Paris cathedral after the fire.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Projected_restoration_of_the_spire">Projected restoration of the spire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Projected restoration of the spire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Notre_dame_paris_august_2019.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Notre_dame_paris_august_2019.jpg/220px-Notre_dame_paris_august_2019.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Notre_dame_paris_august_2019.jpg/330px-Notre_dame_paris_august_2019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Notre_dame_paris_august_2019.jpg/440px-Notre_dame_paris_august_2019.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Notre_dame_paris_august_2019.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Notre-Dame with restoration scaffolding</div></div></div>
<p>Immediately after the fire, President Macron promised that Notre-Dame would be restored, and called for the work to be completed within five years.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> An international architectural competition was also announced to redesign the spire and roof.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The hasty spire competition announcement drew immediate criticism in the international press from heritage academics and professionals who faulted the French government for being too narrowly focused on quickly building a new spire, and neglecting to frame its response more holistically as an inclusive social process encompassing the whole building and its long term users.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> A new law was immediately drafted to make Notre Dame exempt from <a href="/wiki/Conservation_and_restoration_of_cultural_heritage" title="Conservation and restoration of cultural heritage">existing heritage laws and procedures</a>, which prompted an open letter to President Macron signed by over 1,170 heritage experts urging respect for existing regulations.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The law, which passed on May 11, 2019, was hotly debated in the <a href="/wiki/French_National_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="French National Assembly">French National Assembly</a>, with opponents accusing Macron's administration of using Notre-Dame self-servingly for political grandstanding, and defenders arguing the need for expediency and tax breaks to encourage philanthropic giving.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>President Macron suggested he was open to a "contemporary architectural gesture". Even before the competition rules were announced, architects around the world offered suggestions: the proposals included a three hundred foot spire made of carbon fiber, covered with gold leaf; a roof built of stained glass; a greenhouse; a garden with trees, open to the sky; and a column of light pointed upwards. A poll published in the French newspaper <i>Le Figaro</i> on 8 May 2019 showed that fifty-five percent of French respondents wanted a spire identical to the original. French culture minister <a href="/wiki/Franck_Riester" title="Franck Riester">Franck Riester</a> promised that the restoration "will not be hasty."<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
On 16 July 2019, the French Parliament passed a law requiring that the Cathedral be rebuilt exactly as it appeared before the fire.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Towers_and_the_spire">Towers and the spire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Towers and the spire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spire_of_Notre_Dame_de_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Spire of Notre Dame de Paris">Spire of Notre Dame de Paris</a></div>

</p>

		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 295.33333333333px"><div style="width: 295.33333333333px">

			<div class="gallerytext">

</p>

		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The two towers are 69 meters (226&#160;ft) high, and were the tallest structures in Paris until the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_Tower" title="Eiffel Tower">Eiffel Tower</a> in 1889. The towers were the last major element of the cathedral to be constructed. The south tower was built first, between 1220 and 1240, and the north tower between 1235 and 1250. The newer north tower is slightly larger, as can be seen when they are viewed from directly in front of the church. The <i>contrefort</i> or buttress of the north tower is also larger.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The north tower was accessible to visitors by a stairway, whose entrance was on the north side of the tower. The stairway has 387 steps, and has a stop at the Gothic hall at the level of the rose window, where visitors could look over the parvis and see a collection of paintings and sculpture from earlier periods of the cathedral's history.
</p><p>The ten bells of the cathedral are located in the south tower. (see <i><a href="#Bells">Bells</a></i> below)
</p><p>A lead-roofed water reservoir between the two towers&#8205;&#8212;&#8204;behind the colonnade and the gallery and before the nave and the <i>pignon</i>&#8205;&#8212;&#8204;provides water for firefighting.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The cathedral's <a href="/wiki/Fl%C3%A8che" title="Flèche">flèche</a> (or spire), which was destroyed in the April 2019 fire,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> was located over the transept. The original spire was constructed in the 13th century, probably between 1220 and 1230. It was battered, weakened and bent by the wind over five centuries, and finally was removed in 1786. During the 19th-century restoration, Eugène Viollet-le-Duc decided to recreate it, making a new version of oak covered with lead. The entire spire weighed 750 tons.
</p><p>Following Viollet-le-Duc's plans, the spire was surrounded by copper statues of the twelve <a href="/wiki/Apostles" title="Apostles">Apostles</a>&#8205;&#8212;&#8204;a group of three at each point of the compass. In front of each group is an animal symbolising one of the four evangelists: a winged ox for Saint Luke,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> a lion for Saint Mark, an eagle for Saint John and an angel for Saint Matthew. Just days prior to the fire, the statues were removed for restoration.<sup id="cite_ref-Buncombe_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buncombe-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> While in place, they had faced outwards towards Paris, except one: the statue of Saint Thomas, the patron saint of architects, faced the spire, and had the features of Viollet-le-Duc.
</p><p>The rooster weathervane atop the spire contained three relics: a tiny piece from the <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Thorns" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown of Thorns">Crown of Thorns</a> in the cathedral treasury, and relics of Saint <a href="/wiki/Denis" title="Denis">Denis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Genevieve" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Genevieve">Saint Genevieve</a>, patron saints of Paris. They were placed there in 1935 by Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Jean_Verdier" title="Jean Verdier">Jean Verdier</a>, to protect the congregation from lightning or other harm. The rooster with relics intact was recovered in the rubble shortly after the 2019 fire.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Iconography_.E2.80.93_the_.22poor_people.27s_book.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Iconography_–_the_&quot;poor_people's_book&quot;">Iconography – the "poor people's book"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Iconography – the &quot;poor people&#039;s book&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 149.33333333333px"><div style="width: 149.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 147.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Portail_du_Jugement_dernier_-_d%C3%A9tail_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Portail_du_Jugement_dernier_-_d%C3%A9tail_01.jpg/221px-Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Portail_du_Jugement_dernier_-_d%C3%A9tail_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="148" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Portail_du_Jugement_dernier_-_d%C3%A9tail_01.jpg/332px-Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Portail_du_Jugement_dernier_-_d%C3%A9tail_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Portail_du_Jugement_dernier_-_d%C3%A9tail_01.jpg/443px-Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Portail_du_Jugement_dernier_-_d%C3%A9tail_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2951" data-file-height="4000" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The serpent tempts Adam and Eve; part of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Last Judgment</a> on the central portal of west façade
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 277.33333333333px"><div style="width: 277.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 275.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:DevilCentralGateNotreDameParis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/DevilCentralGateNotreDameParis.jpg/413px-DevilCentralGateNotreDameParis.jpg" decoding="async" width="276" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/DevilCentralGateNotreDameParis.jpg/619px-DevilCentralGateNotreDameParis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/DevilCentralGateNotreDameParis.jpg/825px-DevilCentralGateNotreDameParis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1706" data-file-height="1241" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
