</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 146.66666666667px"><div style="width: 146.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 144.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chimera_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris,_26_April_2014.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Chimera_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_26_April_2014.jpg/217px-Chimera_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_26_April_2014.jpg" decoding="async" width="145" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Chimera_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_26_April_2014.jpg/325px-Chimera_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_26_April_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Chimera_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_26_April_2014.jpg/434px-Chimera_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris%2C_26_April_2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1716" data-file-height="2373" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Strix_(mythology)" title="Strix (mythology)">strix</a> on the west façade
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 150px"><div style="width: 150px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 148px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Notre-Dame_Rzygacze.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Notre-Dame_Rzygacze.JPG/222px-Notre-Dame_Rzygacze.JPG" decoding="async" width="148" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Notre-Dame_Rzygacze.JPG/333px-Notre-Dame_Rzygacze.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Notre-Dame_Rzygacze.JPG/445px-Notre-Dame_Rzygacze.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2227" data-file-height="3004" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Gargoyle" title="Gargoyle">Gargoyles</a> were the rainspouts of the Cathedral
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 117.33333333333px"><div style="width: 117.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 115.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gargoyle_Point_of_View_(3575829233).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Gargoyle_Point_of_View_%283575829233%29.jpg/173px-Gargoyle_Point_of_View_%283575829233%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="116" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Gargoyle_Point_of_View_%283575829233%29.jpg/260px-Gargoyle_Point_of_View_%283575829233%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Gargoyle_Point_of_View_%283575829233%29.jpg/346px-Gargoyle_Point_of_View_%283575829233%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1995" data-file-height="3456" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 288.66666666667px"><div style="width: 288.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 286.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alegor%C3%ADa_de_la_alquimia_en_Notre-Dame.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Alegor%C3%ADa_de_la_alquimia_en_Notre-Dame.jpg/430px-Alegor%C3%ADa_de_la_alquimia_en_Notre-Dame.jpg" decoding="async" width="287" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Alegor%C3%ADa_de_la_alquimia_en_Notre-Dame.jpg/645px-Alegor%C3%ADa_de_la_alquimia_en_Notre-Dame.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Alegor%C3%ADa_de_la_alquimia_en_Notre-Dame.jpg/860px-Alegor%C3%ADa_de_la_alquimia_en_Notre-Dame.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2139" data-file-height="1492" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Allegory of <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a>, central portal
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The Gothic cathedral was a <i><a href="/wiki/Poor_Man%27s_Bible" title="Poor Man&#39;s Bible">liber pauperum</a></i>, a "poor people's book", covered with sculpture vividly illustrating biblical stories, for the vast majority of parishioners who were illiterate. To add to the effect, all of the sculpture on the façades was originally painted and gilded.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tympanum_(architecture)" title="Tympanum (architecture)">tympanum</a> over the central portal on the west façade, facing the square, vividly illustrates the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Last Judgment</a>, with figures of sinners being led off to hell, and good Christians taken to heaven. The sculpture of the right portal shows the coronation of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a>, and the left portal shows the lives of saints who were important to Parisians, particularly <a href="/wiki/Saint_Anne" title="Saint Anne">Saint Anne</a>, the mother of the Virgin Mary.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The exteriors of cathedrals and other Gothic churches were also decorated with sculptures of a variety of fabulous and frightening <a href="/wiki/Grotesque_(architecture)" title="Grotesque (architecture)">grotesques</a> or monsters. These included the <a href="/wiki/Gargoyle" title="Gargoyle">gargoyle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chimera_(mythology)" title="Chimera (mythology)">chimera</a>, a mythical hybrid creature which usually had the body of a lion and the head of a goat, and the <a href="/wiki/Strix_(mythology)" title="Strix (mythology)">Strix</a> or stryge, a creature resembling an <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owl</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bat</a>, which was said to eat human flesh. The <i>strix</i> appeared in classical Roman literature; it was described by the Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, who was widely read in the Middle Ages, as a large-headed bird with transfixed eyes, rapacious beak, and greyish white wings.<sup id="cite_ref-fasti_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fasti-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> They were part of the visual message for the illiterate worshipers, symbols of the evil and danger that threatened those who did not follow the teachings of the church.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler201897–99_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler201897–99-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gargoyle" title="Gargoyle">gargoyles</a>, which were added in about 1240, had a more practical purpose. They were the rain spouts of the cathedral, designed to divide the torrent of water which poured from the roof after rain, and to project it outwards as far as possible from the buttresses and the walls and windows where it might erode the mortar binding the stone. To produce many thin streams rather than a torrent of water, a large number of gargoyles were used, so they were also designed to be a decorative element of the architecture. The rainwater ran from the roof into lead gutters, then down channels on the flying buttresses, then along a channel cut in the back of the gargoyle and out of the mouth away from the cathedral.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Amid all the religious figures, some of the sculptural decoration was devoted to illustrating medieval science and philosophy. The central portal of the west façade is decorated with carved figures holding circular plaques with symbols of transformation taken from alchemy. The central pillar of the central door of Notre-Dame features a statue of a woman on a throne holding a scepter in her left hand, and in her right hand, two books, one open (symbol of public knowledge), and the other closed (esoteric knowledge), along with a ladder with seven steps, symbolizing the seven steps alchemists followed in their scientific quest of trying to transform ordinary metals into gold.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWenzler201897–99_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWenzler201897–99-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many of the statues, particularly the grotesques, were removed from façade in the 17th and 18th century, or were destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>. They were replaced with figures in the Gothic style, designed by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a>, during the 19th century restoration.
</p>
<h2><span id="Stained_glass_.E2.80.93_rose_windows"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Stained_glass_–_rose_windows">Stained glass – rose windows</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Stained glass – rose windows">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268.66666666667px"><div style="width: 268.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 266.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:2016-02-23_15-56-57_paris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/2016-02-23_15-56-57_paris.jpg/400px-2016-02-23_15-56-57_paris.jpg" decoding="async" width="267" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/2016-02-23_15-56-57_paris.jpg/600px-2016-02-23_15-56-57_paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/2016-02-23_15-56-57_paris.jpg/800px-2016-02-23_15-56-57_paris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The earliest rose window, on the west façade (about 1225)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 268px"><div style="width: 268px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 266px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_Westliche_Rosette_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_Westliche_Rosette_3.jpg/399px-Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_Westliche_Rosette_3.jpg" decoding="async" width="266" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_Westliche_Rosette_3.jpg/598px-Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_Westliche_Rosette_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_Westliche_Rosette_3.jpg/797px-Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_Westliche_Rosette_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4273" data-file-height="3218" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The west rose window (about 1225)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px"><div style="width: 202px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg/300px-Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg/450px-Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg/600px-Gothic-Rayonnant_Rose-6.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3574" data-file-height="3574" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>North rose window (about 1250)
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 196px"><div style="width: 196px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 194px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_S%C3%BCdliche_Rosette_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_S%C3%BCdliche_Rosette_1.jpg/291px-Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_S%C3%BCdliche_Rosette_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="194" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_S%C3%BCdliche_Rosette_1.jpg/437px-Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_S%C3%BCdliche_Rosette_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_S%C3%BCdliche_Rosette_1.jpg/582px-Paris_Cath%C3%A9drale_Notre-Dame_Innen_S%C3%BCdliche_Rosette_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2789" data-file-height="2875" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>North rose window including lower 18 vertical windows
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul>
<p>The stained glass windows of Notre-Dame, particularly the three <a href="/wiki/Rose_windows" class="mw-redirect" title="Rose windows">rose windows</a>, are among the most famous features of the cathedral. The west rose window, over the portals, was the first and smallest of the roses in Notre-Dame. It is 9.6 metres in diameter, and was made in about 1225, with the pieces of glass set in a thick circular stone frame. None of the original glass remains in this window; it was recreated in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The two transept windows are larger and contain a greater proportion of glass than the rose on the west façade, because the new system of buttresses made the nave walls thinner and stronger. The north rose was created in about 1250, and the south rose in about 1260. The south rose in the transept is particularly notable for its size and artistry. It is 12.9 metres in diameter; with the <i>claire-voie</i> surrounding it, a total of 19 metres. It was given to the Cathedral by King <a href="/wiki/Louis_IX_of_France" title="Louis IX of France">Louis IX of France</a>, known as Saint Louis.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The south rose has 94 medallions, arranged in four circles, depicting scenes from the life of Christ and those who witnessed his time on earth. The inner circle has twelve medallions showing the twelve apostles. (During later restorations, some of these original medallions were moved to circles farther out). The next two circles depict celebrated martyrs and virgins. The fourth circle shows twenty angels, as well as saints important to Paris, notably Saint <a href="/wiki/Denis" title="Denis">Denis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_the_Virgin" title="Margaret the Virgin">Margaret the Virgin</a> with a dragon, and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Eustace" title="Saint Eustace">Saint Eustace</a>. The third and fourth circles also have some depictions of <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> subjects. The third circle has some medallions with scenes from the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> Gospel of Matthew which date from the last quarter of the 12th century. These are the oldest glass in the window.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additional scenes in the corners around the rose window include Jesus' <a href="/wiki/The_Harrowing_of_Hell" class="mw-redirect" title="The Harrowing of Hell">Descent into Hell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve" title="Adam and Eve">Adam and Eve</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">Resurrection of Christ</a>. <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Saint Peter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Paul">Saint Paul</a> are at the bottom of the window, and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Magdalene" title="Mary Magdalene">Mary Magdalene</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John the Apostle</a> at the top.
</p><p>Above the rose was a window depicting Christ triumphant seated in the sky, surrounded by his <a href="/wiki/Apostles" title="Apostles">Apostles</a>. Below are sixteen windows with painted images of Prophets. These were not part of the original window; they were painted during the restoration in the 19th century by Alfred Gérenthe, under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a>, based upon a similar window at <a href="/wiki/Chartres_Cathedral" title="Chartres Cathedral">Chartres Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The south rose had a difficult history. In 1543 it was damaged by the settling of the masonry walls, and not restored until 1725–1727. It was seriously damaged in the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution_of_1830" class="mw-redirect" title="French Revolution of 1830">French Revolution of 1830</a>. Rioters burned the residence of the archbishop, next to the cathedral, and many of the panes were destroyed. The window was entirely rebuilt by Viollet-le-Duc in 1861. He rotated the window by fifteen degrees to give it a clear vertical and horizontal axis, and replaced the destroyed pieces of glass with new glass in the same style. The window today contains both medieval and 19th century glass.<sup id="cite_ref-notredamedeparis_99-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-notredamedeparis-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1960s, after three decades of debate, it was decided to replace many of the 19th-century grisaille windows in the nave designed by Viollet-le-Duc with new windows. The new windows, made by <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Le_Chevallier" title="Jacques Le Chevallier">Jacques Le Chevallier</a>, are without human figures and use abstract <i><a href="/wiki/Grisaille" title="Grisaille">grisaille</a></i> designs and color to try to recreate the luminosity of the Cathedral's interior in the 13th century.
</p><p>The 2019 fire left the three great medieval rose windows essentially intact, but with some damage.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The rector of the Cathedral noted that one rose window would have to be dismantled, as it was unstable and at risk.<sup id="cite_ref-huffpost_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffpost-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Most of the other damaged windows were of much less historical value.<sup id="cite_ref-huffpost_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffpost-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crypt">Crypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Crypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:La_crypte_arch%C3%A9ologique_du_Parvis_de_Notre-Dame_(Paris)_(8274683584).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/La_crypte_arch%C3%A9ologique_du_Parvis_de_Notre-Dame_%28Paris%29_%288274683584%29.jpg/220px-La_crypte_arch%C3%A9ologique_du_Parvis_de_Notre-Dame_%28Paris%29_%288274683584%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/La_crypte_arch%C3%A9ologique_du_Parvis_de_Notre-Dame_%28Paris%29_%288274683584%29.jpg/330px-La_crypte_arch%C3%A9ologique_du_Parvis_de_Notre-Dame_%28Paris%29_%288274683584%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/La_crypte_arch%C3%A9ologique_du_Parvis_de_Notre-Dame_%28Paris%29_%288274683584%29.jpg/440px-La_crypte_arch%C3%A9ologique_du_Parvis_de_Notre-Dame_%28Paris%29_%288274683584%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1366" data-file-height="900" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:La_crypte_arch%C3%A9ologique_du_Parvis_de_Notre-Dame_(Paris)_(8274683584).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Archaeological Crypt of Notre-Dame de Paris</div></div></div>
<p>The Archaeological Crypt (Crypte archéologique de l'île de la Cité) was created in 1965 to protect a range of historical ruins discovered during construction work and spanning from the earliest settlement in Paris to the modern day. The crypt is managed by the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Carnavalet" title="Musée Carnavalet">Musée Carnavalet</a>, and contains a large exhibit, detailed models of the architecture of different time periods, and how they can be viewed within the ruins. The main feature still visible is the under-floor heating installed during the Roman occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organ">Organ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Organ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Organ_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Organ_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris.jpg/220px-Organ_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="328" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Organ_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris.jpg/330px-Organ_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Organ_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris.jpg/440px-Organ_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2478" data-file-height="3700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Organ_of_Notre-Dame_de_Paris.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The organ of Notre-Dame de Paris</div></div></div>
<p>One of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Pipe_organ" title="Pipe organ">organs</a> at Notre-Dame, built in 1403 by Friedrich Schambantz, was replaced between 1730 and 1738 by <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Thierry" title="François Thierry">François Thierry</a>. During the restoration of the cathedral by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Viollet-le-Duc" title="Eugène Viollet-le-Duc">Eugène Viollet-le-Duc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristide_Cavaill%C3%A9-Coll" title="Aristide Cavaillé-Coll">Aristide Cavaillé-Coll</a> built a new organ, using pipe work from the former instruments. The organ was dedicated in 1868.
</p><p>In 1904, Charles Mutin modified and added several <a href="/wiki/Organ_stop" title="Organ stop">stops</a>; in 1924, an electric blower was installed. An extensive restoration and cleaning was carried out by Joseph Beuchet in 1932. Between 1959 and 1963, the mechanical action with <a href="/wiki/Barker_lever" title="Barker lever">Barker levers</a> was replaced with an electric action by Jean Hermann, and a new organ console was installed.
</p><p>During the following years, the stoplist was gradually modified by Robert Boisseau (who added three <a href="/wiki/En_chamade" title="En chamade">chamade stops</a>: 8′, 4′, and 2′/16′ in 1968) and Jean-Loup Boisseau after 1975, respectively. In autumn 1983, the electric combination system was disconnected due to short-circuit risk.
</p><p>Between 1990 and 1992, Jean-Loup Boisseau, Bertrand Cattiaux, Philippe Émeriau, Michel Giroud, and the Société Synaptel revised and augmented the instrument throughout. A new console was installed, using the stop knobs, pedal and manual keyboards, foot pistons and balance pedals from the Jean Hermann console. Between 2012 and 2014, Bertrand Cattiaux and Pascal Quoirin restored, cleaned, and modified the organ. The stop and key action was upgraded, a new console was built, (again using the stop keys, pedal board, foot pistons and balance pedals of the 1992 console), a new enclosed division ("Résonnance expressive", using pipework from the former "Petite Pédale" by Boisseau, which can now be used as a floating division), the organ case and the façade pipes were restored, and a general tuning was carried out. The current organ has 115 stops (156 ranks) on five manuals and pedal, and more than 8,000 <a href="/wiki/Organ_pipe" title="Organ pipe">pipes</a>.
</p>

</div>
<p>Couplers: II/I, III/I, IV/I, V/I; III/II, IV/II, V/II; IV/III, V/III; V/IV, Octave grave général, inversion Positif/Grand-orgue, Tirasses (Grand-orgue, Positif, Récit, Solo, Grand-Chœur en 8; Grand-Orgue en 4, Positif en 4, Récit en 4, Solo en 4, Grand-Chœur en 4), Sub- und Super octave couplers and Unison Off for all manuals (Octaves graves, octaves aiguës, annulation 8′). Octaves aiguës Pédalier.
Additional features: Coupure Pédalier. Coupure Chamade. Appel Résonnance. Sostenuto for all manuals and the pedal. Cancel buttons for each division. 50,000 combinations (5,000 groups each). Replay system.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organists">Organists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Organists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The position of titular organist ("head" or "chief" organist; French: <i>titulaires des grands orgues</i>) at Notre-Dame is considered one of the most prestigious organist posts in France, along with the post of titular organist of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Sulpice_(Paris)" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint-Sulpice (Paris)">Saint Sulpice</a> in Paris, Cavaillé-Coll's largest instrument.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bells">Bells</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bells">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_bells_in_Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="List of bells in Notre-Dame de Paris">List of bells in Notre-Dame de Paris</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="29.280362811791" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Bourdon_Marie_(Notre-Dame_de_Paris)_1.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/3/35/Bourdon_Marie_%28Notre-Dame_de_Paris%29_1.ogg/Bourdon_Marie_%28Notre-Dame_de_Paris%29_1.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="160872" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/Bourdon_Marie_%28Notre-Dame_de_Paris%29_1.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (305 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="305287" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bourdon_Marie_(Notre-Dame_de_Paris)_1.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The new bell, Marie, ringing in the nave</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Les_nouvelles_cloches_-_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Les_nouvelles_cloches_-_001.jpg/220px-Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Les_nouvelles_cloches_-_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Les_nouvelles_cloches_-_001.jpg/330px-Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Les_nouvelles_cloches_-_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Les_nouvelles_cloches_-_001.jpg/440px-Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Les_nouvelles_cloches_-_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4694" data-file-height="3130" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Notre-Dame_de_Paris_-_Les_nouvelles_cloches_-_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The nine bells of Notre-Dame de Paris Cathedral on public display in the nave in February 2013 (From left to right) Jean-Marie, Maurice, Benoît-Joseph, Étienne, Marcel, Denis, Anne-Geneviève, Gabriel and Bourdon Marie</div></div></div>
<p>The cathedral has ten <a href="/wiki/Church_bell" title="Church bell">bells</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bourdon_(bell)" title="Bourdon (bell)">bourdon</a>, called Emmanuel, weighing at 13 tons<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and tuned to F sharp, has been an accompaniment to some of France's major historical events since its first 15th-century casting<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, such as the coronation of French kings, papal visits, and the end of conflicts such as <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. It also rings in times of sorrow like for the funerals of the French heads of state, tragedies such as the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">terrorist attacks on 11 September 2001</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and on special holidays like <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus" title="Ascension of Jesus">Ascension</a>. It was recast at the request of <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">King Louis XIV</a> in 1681, and managed to escape the events of the 1789 <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>. According to bell ringers and musicians, it is still one of the most beautiful sound vessels and one of the most remarkable of its kind in Europe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>There were also four bells that replaced those destroyed in the French Revolution. Placed at the top of the North Tower in 1856, these ring daily for basic services, the Angelus and the chiming of the hours. The first of these bells, named Angélique-Françoise, weighs in at 1,915&#160;kg and is tuned to C sharp; the next bell is named Antoinette-Charlotte, weighing in at 1,335&#160;kg and tuned to D sharp. Then there is the bell named Jacinthe-Jeanne weighing in at 925&#160;kg tuned to F and the fourth bell named Denise-David weighs 767&#160;kg and just like the Grand Bell Emmanuel, it is tuned to F sharp. A few years later, in 1867, a <a href="/wiki/Carillon" title="Carillon">carillon</a> of three bells in the spire with two chimes that linked to the monumental clock were put in place and another three bells were positioned in the actual structure of Notre-Dame itself, so that they could be heard inside. However, unfortunately, these are at present mute, although a project is currently being looked at, and hopefully will be put into place, in order to restore the Carillon to its former glory. The four bells that were put in place in 1856 are now stored, as of February 2012.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paris_for_SIAM_PP_2016_(36378821523).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Paris_for_SIAM_PP_2016_%2836378821523%29.jpg/220px-Paris_for_SIAM_PP_2016_%2836378821523%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Paris_for_SIAM_PP_2016_%2836378821523%29.jpg/330px-Paris_for_SIAM_PP_2016_%2836378821523%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Paris_for_SIAM_PP_2016_%2836378821523%29.jpg/440px-Paris_for_SIAM_PP_2016_%2836378821523%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3936" data-file-height="5248" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paris_for_SIAM_PP_2016_(36378821523).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bourdon Emmanuel</div></div></div>
<p>About a year later, a new set of eight bells for the North Tower of Notre-Dame was being produced, along with a Grand Bell for the South Tower, just as there were originally before most were destroyed during the French Revolution. The construction of bells is one of accuracy and precision to obtain the desired sound and the work has been entrusted to two separate companies, one in France for the eight bells and one in Belgium for the Grand Bell. Each of the new bells is named with names that have been chosen to pay tribute to saints and others who have shaped the life of Paris and the Notre-Dame.
</p><p>Emmanuel is accompanied by another large bell in the south tower called Marie. At six tonnes and playing a G Sharp, Marie is the second largest bell in the cathedral. Marie is also called a Little Bourdon (petite bourdon) or a drone bell&#160;because it is located alongside Emmanuel in the south tower. Built in a foundry in The Netherlands, it has engravings as a very distinctive feature, something which is different compared to the other bells. The phrases "Je vous salue Marie," in French, and "Via viatores quaerit," in Latin, which mean "Hail Mary" (where the bell gets its name from the <a href="/wiki/Mary,_mother_of_Jesus" title="Mary, mother of Jesus">Virgin Mary</a>) and "The way is looking for travellers". Below the phrase appears an image of the Baby <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> and his parents surrounded by stars and a relief with the <a href="/wiki/Adoration_of_the_Magi" title="Adoration of the Magi">Adoration of the Magi</a>. It is in charge of the Small Solennel, which is similar to the Great Solennel except that the ringing peal starts with the bourdon and the eight bells in the north tower. This ring is heard on only 1 January (New Year's Day) at the stroke of midnight and it replaces Emmanuel for international events. Like Emmanuel, the bells are used to mark specific moments such as the arrival at the Cathedral of the body of the deceased Archbishop of Paris.
</p><p>In the North Tower, there are eight bells varying in size from largest to smallest. Gabriel is the largest bell there; it weighs four tons and plays an A sharp. It is named after <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">the Archangel Gabriel</a>, who announced the birth of Jesus to the Virgin Mary. Built in a bell foundry outside Paris in 2013, it also chimes the hour through the day. Like Emmanuel and Marie, Gabriel is used to mark specific events. It is used mainly for masses on Sundays in ordinary times and some solemnities falling during the week in the Plenum North. It shows 40 circular lines representing the 40 days Jesus spent in the desert and the 40 years of Moses' crossing the Sinai.
</p><p>Anne-Geneviève is the second largest bell in the North Tower and the fourth largest bell in the cathedral. Named after two saints: <a href="/wiki/Saint_Anne" title="Saint Anne">St. Anne</a>, Mary's mother, and <a href="/wiki/Genevieve" title="Genevieve">St. Geneviève</a> the patron saint of Paris, it plays a B and it weighs three tons. It has three circular lines that represent the Holy Trinity and three theological virtues. Like Emmanuel, Marie and Gabriel, Anne-Genevieve is used to mark specific moments such as the opening of the doors to the <a href="/wiki/Palm_Sunday" title="Palm Sunday">Palm Sunday</a> mass or the body of the deceased Archbishop of Paris. Also it is the only bell that does not participate in a chime called the Angelus Domini, which happens in the summer at 8am, noon and 8pm (or 9am, noon and 9pm).
</p><p>Denis&#160;is the third largest bell in the North Tower and fifth largest bell in the cathedral. It is named after <a href="/wiki/Denis" title="Denis">St. Denis</a>, the martyr who was also the first bishop of Paris around the year 250; it weighs 2 tons and plays a C sharp. This bell includes the third phrase of the Angelus, "Behold the handmaid of the Lord". There are also seven circular lines representing the gifts of the Holy Spirit and the seven Sacraments.
</p><p>Marcel&#160;is the fourth largest bell in the North Tower and sixth largest bell in the cathedral. It&#160;is named after the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcel_of_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Marcel of Paris (page does not exist)">9th bishop of Paris</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marcel_de_Paris" class="extiw" title="fr:Marcel de Paris">fr</a>&#93;</span>. It plays a D sharp and weighs 1.9 tons. It is named after Saint Marcel, the ninth bishop of Paris, known for his tireless service to the poor and sick in the fifth century. The bell that bears his name as a tribute has engraved upon it the fourth sentence of the Angelus, "Be it done unto me according to Thy word".
</p><p>Étienne is the fifth largest bell in the North Tower and seventh largest bell in the cathedral. It is named after St. Étienne (English <a href="/wiki/Saint_Stephen" title="Saint Stephen">St. Stephen</a>), the first Christian martyr. It plays an E sharp and weighs 1.5 tons with its most prominent feature being its gold stripe slightly above the nameplate.
</p><p>Benoît-Joseph is the sixth largest bell in the North Tower and eighth largest bell in the cathedral. The bell is named in honor of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XVI" title="Pope Benedict XVI">Pope Benedict XVI</a>, using the French version of his <a href="/wiki/Papal_name" title="Papal name">pontifical name</a> combined with his given name (Joseph). It plays an F and weighs 1.3 tons. It has two silver stripes above the skirt and one silver stripe above the nameplate. This bell is used for weddings and sometimes chimes the hour replacing Gabriel, most likely on a chime called the <a href="/wiki/Hail_Mary" title="Hail Mary">Ave Maria</a>.
</p><p>Maurice is the seventh largest bell in the North Tower and second smallest in the cathedral. It is named after <a href="/wiki/Maurice_de_Sully" title="Maurice de Sully">Maurice de Sully</a>, the bishop of Paris who laid the first stone for the construction of the Cathedral. It includes the inscription, "Pray for us, Holy Mother of God". It plays a G sharp and weighs one ton. It has two gray stripes below the nameplate. This bell is used for weddings.
</p><p>Jean Marie is the smallest bell of the cathedral. Unlike Benoît-Joseph and Anne-Geneviève which have two names, it is named after Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Jean-Marie_Lustiger" title="Jean-Marie Lustiger">Jean-Marie Lustiger</a>, Paris' bishop from 1981 until 2005, and on it is engraved the eighth and last sentence of the Angelus: "that we might be made worthy of the promises of Christ". It plays an A sharp and weighs 0.780 tons. It has a small gray stripe above the skirt. This bell is also used for weddings.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ownership">Ownership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ownership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Until the French Revolution, Notre-Dame was the property of the Archbishop of Paris and therefore the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>. It was nationalized on 2 November 1789 and since then has been the property of the French state.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Under the Concordat of 1801, use of the cathedral was returned to the Church, but not ownership. Legislation from 1833 and 1838 clarified that cathedrals were maintained at the expense of the French government. This was reaffirmed in <a href="/wiki/1905_French_law_on_the_Separation_of_the_Churches_and_the_State" title="1905 French law on the Separation of the Churches and the State">the 1905 law on the separation of Church and State</a>, designating the Catholic Church as having the exclusive right to use it for religious purposes in perpetuity. Notre-Dame is one of seventy historic churches in France with this status. The archdiocese is responsible for paying the employees, for security, heating and cleaning, and for ensuring that the cathedral is open free to visitors. The archdiocese does not receive subsidies from the French state.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Notre-Dame_de_Paris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The name <i>Notre Dame</i>, meaning "<a href="/wiki/Blessed_Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Blessed Virgin Mary">Our Lady</a>" was frequently used in <a href="/wiki/Notre_Dame_(disambiguation)#Churches_and_cathedrals" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Notre Dame (disambiguation)">names of churches</a> including the cathedrals of <a href="/wiki/Chartres_Cathedral" title="Chartres Cathedral">Chartres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reims_Cathedral" title="Reims Cathedral">Rheims</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rouen_Cathedral" title="Rouen Cathedral">Rouen</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Excavations have failed to accurately determine the architectural history of the <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele_de_la_Cit%C3%A9" title="Île de la Cité">Île de la Cité</a>. It appears that Bishop Sully entirely dug out the foundations of the early Christian basilica so as to found Notre-Dame on the bedrock under the island.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The growth of the population of <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> and other French cities was characteristic of Western Europe during the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_of_the_12th_century" title="Renaissance of the 12th century">Renaissance of the 12th century</a>. It is thought that the population of Paris grew from 25,000 in 1180 to 50,000 in 1220, making it the largest European city outside of Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
